  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d8de2ef8-1950-4606-b97f-5e1e160ed888'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12388335542738593020">C.S. Lewis said that upon reading this astonishing 19th-century fairy tale he "had crossed a great frontier," and numerous others both before and since have felt similarly.<br/><br/>In MacDonald's fairy tales, both those for children and (like this one) those for adults, the "fairy land" clearly represents the spiritual world, or our own world revealed in all of its depth and meanin</span>
  <span id="freeText12388335542738593020" style="display:none">C.S. Lewis said that upon reading this astonishing 19th-century fairy tale he "had crossed a great frontier," and numerous others both before and since have felt similarly.<br /><br />In MacDonald's fairy tales, both those for children and (like this one) those for adults, the "fairy land" clearly represents the spiritual world, or our own world revealed in all of its depth and meaning. At times almost forthrightly allegorical, at other times richly dreamlike (and indeed having a close connection to the symbolic world of dreams), this story of a young man who finds himself on a long journey through a land of fantasy is more truly the story of the spiritual quest that is at the core of his life's work, a quest that must end with the ultimate surrender of the self.<br /><br />The glory of MacDonald's work is that this surrender is both hard won (or lost!) and yet rippling with joy when at last experienced. As the narrator says of a heavenly woman in this tale, "She knew something too good to be told." One senses the same of the author himself.<br /><br />Newly designed and typeset in a modern 6-by-9-inch format by Waking Lion Press.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12388335542738593020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_1401419617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1401419617" />
    <a title="Cindy Rollins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3480406-cindy-rollins"><img alt="Cindy Rollins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379169069p2/3480406.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1401419617?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cindy Rollins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cindy Rollins" href="/user/show/3480406-cindy-rollins">Cindy Rollins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3480406-cindy-rollins?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1401419617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13483210379196990100">The first time I read this I was a newly married 18 yo. My husband was taking a class at college titled Oxford Christians and I may as well have taken the class myself because I read every single life-changing book Dr. Kay Ludwigson assigned. And of all the books by all those wonderful Inklings and hangers-on, this book, Phantastes, captured my imagination and began my love of George MacDonald in a unique way. <br/><br/>I loved this book. Ordo Amoris. <br/><br/>They say the brain has definite patterns of nostalgia </span>
  <span id="freeText13483210379196990100" style="display:none">The first time I read this I was a newly married 18 yo. My husband was taking a class at college titled Oxford Christians and I may as well have taken the class myself because I read every single life-changing book Dr. Kay Ludwigson assigned. And of all the books by all those wonderful Inklings and hangers-on, this book, Phantastes, captured my imagination and began my love of George MacDonald in a unique way. <br /><br />I loved this book. Ordo Amoris. <br /><br />They say the brain has definite patterns of nostalgia so that they can predict your age by what you listen to on the radio. I wonder if that is also true of returning to old book loves? <br /><br />I find myself, more and more, returning to those old loves to see if they are still the bright baubles of my youth. <br /><br />Phantastes still holds up. George MacDonald is still a good man who writes good books and those books still make me want to be good. I am not the passionate 18 yo ready to fix all things wrong anymore. At 18 I was fascinated with the statuesque lady. Perhaps I wanted to be loved like that. This time around I barely noticed her. I deeply understood and was encouraged by the place Anodos had to come to in order to lose his shadow, his self. I long to get to that place where my shadow, my self, taps me on the shoulder and I brush her away without a thought. What care I for her? <br /><br />I finished this book up as I sat in the emergency room with my husband. I was completely sheathed in my self as I hunkered there trying not to notice the horribleness of the humanity around me. Yes, it seemed horrible to me. Those sick, sick people. I wanted to just be me sitting somewhere safe and warm drinking coffee and eating cookies, watching the hummingbirds and listening to Bach. True, good, beautiful ME. But George MacDonald stripped that bare and showed me what the ugliness of my own vision for my own self. I had thought I had made progress in the brushing her aside but there she was in the emergency room hunkered down trying to ignore the back of the tapestry-the place Christ always is. The place where humanity roils and smells and writhes. Christ was there even when I was trying not to be. Forgive me, Father. Forgive me for underestimating the sinfulness of my own self.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13483210379196990100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_564289085" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/564289085" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/564289085?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802860606" href="/book/show/541923.Phantastes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=library-borrow">library-borrow</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=19th-centurylit-late">19th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer564289085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4631647625595155117">I like a good faerie story, a nice romp in fairy lands. I especially like reading older fantasy novels to help this graph I have in my head showing the progression and evolution of fantasy in literature. MacDonald's book here, published in 1857, seemed like a good one to pick up - it's an early fantasy novel with an introduction by C.S. Lewis, possibly the world's first MacDonald fanboy (and OMG he drooled all over MacDonald in that introduction), and this MacDonald guy inspired not only Lewis, </span>
  <span id="freeText4631647625595155117" style="display:none">I like a good faerie story, a nice romp in fairy lands. I especially like reading older fantasy novels to help this graph I have in my head showing the progression and evolution of fantasy in literature. MacDonald's book here, published in 1857, seemed like a good one to pick up - it's an early fantasy novel with an introduction by C.S. Lewis, possibly the world's first MacDonald fanboy (and OMG he drooled all over MacDonald in that introduction), and this MacDonald guy inspired not only Lewis, but also Tolkien, and I like him, so.<br /><br />Here's something I'm not always that great at, and especially right now when I'm sickly: ALLEGORIES. I often <i>suck</i> at reading allegories. When I read Narnia the first time? I was all, "Oh, hey, Aslan the lion is super cute." And then I learned what Narnia and Aslan represented and I was all, "No freaking way!" I had to read it all again to make sense of it all, and even then I was a little skeptical.<br /><br />I went into reading this knowing a bit about MacDonald and that he wrote these allegorical type things. I was a little hesitant, but open to the idea - so many of these writers (like Lewis) write fantastical allegories and somehow in my head I'm trying to make sense of it all because that's just not how my brain works, I'm too scientific or something. I think there are religious stories and then there are fantasy novels, and I don't always get how they can be one and the same without likely getting into an argument with someone. So it's better to just keep my mouth shut.<br /><br />Okay, so the story is fine, but man, it really dragged for me. I don't feel it ever really picked up, and maybe that's because I knew that I was being (for lack of a better word) tricked by MacDonald. I knew that what he was writing was not what he was <i>saying</i> and that made me sort of irate. So I tried to put that aside and just focus on the imagery because MacDonald wrote incredible imagery. But that trickery was beneath it and I couldn't get over it.<br /><br />Plus, there are a lot of songs. Remember Tom Bombadil's songs in Tolkien? Exactly.<br /><br />Whatever, this just didn't work for me. It's not without merit, though, and clearly a lot of writers I do appreciate, respect, or even enjoy were into MacDonald. I have more of his books that I will eventually read, but I'm not particularly looking forward to it.<br /><br />Let's put it this way - this book wasn't worth the overdue library fees I accrued by holding onto it longer than I should have. It's just... it was so <i>short</i> that I thought it would read <i>faster</i> and that was definitely not the case.<br /><br />You can put cheese on broccoli but it's still broccoli, y'know?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4631647625595155117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194331159" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194331159" />
    <a title="Jacob Aitken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5958504-jacob-aitken"><img alt="Jacob Aitken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463699884p2/5958504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194331159?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Aitken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Aitken" href="/user/show/5958504-jacob-aitken">Jacob Aitken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802860606" href="/book/show/541923.Phantastes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5958504-jacob-aitken?shelf=beauty">beauty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5958504-jacob-aitken?shelf=inklings">inklings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5958504-jacob-aitken?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194331159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6412123089114098684">In many ways this really isn't a good book. The style borders on choppy and dense. The story doesn't always flow. MacDonald routinely makes excurses without telling you. But...<br/><br/><br/><br/>The "mythopoeic" prose is its redeeming quality. MaDonald bathed the book in sacramentality. Every leaf, grove, and spring refleted redemption--and MacDonald is a talented enough artist that he can show redemption without telling you redemption (usually). <br/><br/><br/><br/>The story line is simple enough. The protagonists finds himself in "</span>
  <span id="freeText6412123089114098684" style="display:none">In many ways this really isn't a good book. The style borders on choppy and dense. The story doesn't always flow. MacDonald routinely makes excurses without telling you. But...<br /><br /><br /><br />The "mythopoeic" prose is its redeeming quality. MaDonald bathed the book in sacramentality. Every leaf, grove, and spring refleted redemption--and MacDonald is a talented enough artist that he can show redemption without telling you redemption (usually). <br /><br /><br /><br />The story line is simple enough. The protagonists finds himself in "faerie land" and must navigate through trials and temptations, with all the self-discoveries. <br /><br /><br /><br />CS Lewis mentioned this book spoke of a "good death." Seems odd that a Christian (even a heterodox one) like MacDonald would be so preoccupied with Death when most of MacDonald's works ooze "Life." What Lewis means is that MacDonald uses the medium of "death" to kill the old element and make way for the new (Life). This is none other than the Christian story of Baptism, a Baptism that our hero must undergo. <br /><br /><br /><br />Some things I learned about Fairie Land:<br /><br />*Fairy wisdom is sounder than anything we moderns have come up with.<br /><br />*The Arthurian legends are reliable guides to the human conditions and are "true" on more than one level.<br /><br />*Giants prefer to use maces in battle.<br /><br />*Adventures usually happen in Forests.<br /><br /><br /><br />Final thoughts:<br /><br />Sometimes the beauty was so intoxicating that I felt my heart would stop. Accepting what I now do about Plato and Carl Jung, MacDonald plucked an archetypal chord in my soul. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6412123089114098684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

But it struck me as kind of a fairy land hodge podge, only with the hodge parts and the podge parts packed closely tog</span>

But it struck me as kind of a fairy land hodge podge, only with the hodge parts and the podge parts packed closely together by hand. Anyway.</span>

<div id="review_166448551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166448551" />
    <a title="Werner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner"><img alt="Werner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166448551?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Werner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Werner" href="/user/show/903390-werner">Werner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fantasy fans who don't mind Victorian diction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166448551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16668743247285749838">While I read this book several years ago (the 2006 date is a "best guess"), I'd actually started it back in 1990 and didn't finish it at that time. It gets off to kind of a slow start, and one element in the storyline was initially off-putting to me (but no spoilers here!). However, I'm glad I decided to give it a second and fairer chance; it proved to be a solid three-star fantasy that I enjoyed. Basically, it's a coming-of-age tale in a fantasy setting; and it's perhaps the first example in th</span>
  <span id="freeText16668743247285749838" style="display:none">While I read this book several years ago (the 2006 date is a "best guess"), I'd actually started it back in 1990 and didn't finish it at that time. It gets off to kind of a slow start, and one element in the storyline was initially off-putting to me (but no spoilers here!). However, I'm glad I decided to give it a second and fairer chance; it proved to be a solid three-star fantasy that I enjoyed. Basically, it's a coming-of-age tale in a fantasy setting; and it's perhaps the first example in the modern tradition of a child/youth from the real world finding his way into a fantasy world. (Knowing that MacDonald was a favorite author of C. S. Lewis, it isn't hard to see the influence of this work on the idea behind the latter's Narnia series.) There are actually no explicit Christian references in the book, but the author's Christian worldview underlies the strongly moral tone and messages here. Of course, this is a 19th-century work, with Victorian diction throughout; readers who find that problematic will probably enjoy the book less than those of us who don't mind that!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16668743247285749838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2956136631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2956136631" />
    <a title="Neil R. Coulter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22306206-neil-r-coulter"><img alt="Neil R. Coulter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592517121p2/22306206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2956136631?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil R. Coulter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil R. Coulter" href="/user/show/22306206-neil-r-coulter">Neil R. Coulter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781935688150" href="/book/show/36544141-phantastes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22306206-neil-r-coulter?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22306206-neil-r-coulter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22306206-neil-r-coulter?shelf=bedtime-reading-with-kids">bedtime-reading-with-kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2956136631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11104009105798690168"><b>January 2020 review:</b><br/><br/>What can I write about a book like <i>Phantastes</i>? It’s a novel that seems like it wasn’t <i>crafted </i>so much as <i>found</i>, its vignettes—alternately dreamy or nightmarish, adventurous or languid—not invented but simply reported and transmitted through the pages, verbatim visions of the fairy world. It feels like a living book, a story that will respond to me in whatever way I need, depending on where I am and what I need at the moment. I’ve read it twice in the past year, and in hoping </span>
  <span id="freeText11104009105798690168" style="display:none"><b>January 2020 review:</b><br /><br />What can I write about a book like <i>Phantastes</i>? It’s a novel that seems like it wasn’t <i>crafted </i>so much as <i>found</i>, its vignettes—alternately dreamy or nightmarish, adventurous or languid—not invented but simply reported and transmitted through the pages, verbatim visions of the fairy world. It feels like a living book, a story that will respond to me in whatever way I need, depending on where I am and what I need at the moment. I’ve read it twice in the past year, and in hoping to reread it once a year from now on, I expect that it will feel like a slightly different story each time.<br /><br />It’s also a book of books, of connections to other stories I’m very familiar with. Much of it feels like almost the same Wonderland as Lewis Carroll’s—and because of the great friendship between MacDonald and Carroll, that’s a pretty direct connection of influence. <i>Phantastes </i>also reminds me, of course, of C. S. Lewis and J. R. R. Tolkien, where, again, MacDonald is a known influence on both authors. But the book it most reminds me of is a connection I didn’t expect (though probably should have): Michael Ende’s <i>The Neverending Story</i>. Parts of Anodos’s journey into shadow, darkness, and death and then back to life are extremely similar to what Bastian Balthazar Bux goes through when he’s pulled into Fantastica and loses himself before being restored. Both protagonists are cared for by compassionate women who nurture them as though they were children again, helping them to see what love truly means. The connection between MacDonald and Ende shouldn’t have surprised me, because they both draw from the German Romance tradition of fairy tales. The similarities between the books don’t make me love either one less; they are both treasured favorites.<br /><br />I read <i>Phantastes </i>in the 2017 annotated edition, edited by John Pennington and Roderick McGillis. Annotations are almost mandatory for this book, which is full of fragments of other books and poems, and a bit of archaic language that needs clarification. The editors also point out connections to larger literary themes, which enhanced my appreciation of the book. In the back of the volume, the editors have even collected the full (or fuller) versions of a number of the works referenced in <i>Phantastes</i>.<br /><br />I only have two criticisms of the annotated edition. One is that the publication has a very self-published feel, which is disappointing because I’d love for a book like this to be more of an heirloom, not a cheap paperback with adequate but not beautiful formatting. My other disappointment is that the back matter contains a short article about Arthur Hughes’s illustrations for the 1905 edition—and then reprints those illustrations much too small to be really appreciated. Really, each illustration should have been given a full page.<br /><br /><i>Phantastes </i>wouldn’t be to everyone’s liking (its initial reviews in 1858 were mostly negative, too), but for those who have an eye to see what’s in it, and the patience to journey with MacDonald and Anodos, it’s a tremendous experience.<br /><br /><b>October 2019 review:</b><br /><br />It's been a while since I finished a book and immediately wanted to read it again. I'm going to do that with <i>Phantastes</i>, so I won't write much about it until I've been through it one more time. Amazing book.<blockquote>Even yet, I find myself looking round sometimes with anxiety, to see whether my shadow falls right away from the sun or no. I have never yet discovered any inclination to either side. And if I am not unfrequently sad, I yet cast no more of a shade on the earth, than most men who have lived in it as long as I. I have a strange feeling sometimes, that I am a ghost, sent into the world to minister to my fellow men, or, rather, to repair the wrongs I have already done. May the world be brighter for me, at least in those portions of it, where my darkness falls not.<p>Thus I, who set out to find my Ideal, came back rejoicing that I had lost my Shadow. (194–195)</p></blockquote><blockquote>Yet I know that good is coming to me—that good is always coming; though few have at all times the simplicity and the courage to believe it. What we call evil, is the only and best shape, which, for the person and his condition at the time, could be assumed by the best good. (195–196)</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="11104009105798690168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164596620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2696565706780747856">“But Love is such a Mystery<br/>I cannot find it out:<br/>For when I think I’m best resolv’d,<br/>I then am in most doubt.”<br/>(Sir John Suckling)<br/><br/>I have just finished Phantastes and was immediately compelled to put my thoughts to paper. What attracted me to the book was, beside the title, the blurb at the back which said the story is a “fairy tale for adults” and I needed no more persuasion.<br/><br/>The book relates the story of Anodos, a young wealthy man who, on his 21st birthday receives the keys to a mysterious secreta</span>
  <span id="freeText2696565706780747856" style="display:none">“But Love is such a Mystery<br />I cannot find it out:<br />For when I think I’m best resolv’d,<br />I then am in most doubt.”<br />(Sir John Suckling)<br /><br />I have just finished Phantastes and was immediately compelled to put my thoughts to paper. What attracted me to the book was, beside the title, the blurb at the back which said the story is a “fairy tale for adults” and I needed no more persuasion.<br /><br />The book relates the story of Anodos, a young wealthy man who, on his 21st birthday receives the keys to a mysterious secretary which belonged to his father. He opens it and so begins his journey into adulthood. It is really the story of his coming of age through challenges he has to overcome, of joy and love and sadness and despair, for he must go through all of that. <br />His journey takes him to a fantastic land – he meets a birch-tree that is not really a tree, statues that are not really statues, giants and knights and kind old ladies. He is imprisoned but escapes, he fights for a noble cause and wins, he meets all sorts of creatures, good and evil, all meant to make him understand and learn life’s lessons. Learn that sometimes we do harm and are forgiven by those whom we have hurt, that love can be of many ways, that beauty does not equal purity of soul, and friendship has wonderful rewards. Each adventure is meant to teach him something and he comes out of this experience changed, an adult. <br /><br />Although the language was not very easy to read (the book was, after all, published in the mid 1850’s) and I found myself going back to re-read certain passages, the story had a melody which made me want to keep going. Imbued with wonderful bits of poetry and very vividly described scenes, it took me to another world where everything was possible and nothing was left to chance, to a land where beauty goes hand in hand with ugliness and where weeping is the companion of laughter. In other words, life.<br /><br />“Fight on, my men, Sir Andrew sayes, <br />A little Ime hurt, but yett not slaine;<br />Yle but lye downe and bleede awhile, <br />And then Ile rise and fight againe.”<br />(Ballad of Sir Andrew Barton)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2696565706780747856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18412171562949112348">Lyrical, mesmerizing "faerie romance for men and women", thus far this story focuses on Anodos and his epic journey through the dreamlike Fairy Land - but if the reader is looking for tiny winged creatures, he will find them only briefly; Fairy Land is populated with numerous inhabitants who are in fact human, and others appear so but with supernatural qualities. Though society (and rampant marketing) have oversold the idea of a benign parallel world of beauty and frolicking sprites, make no mis</span>
  <span id="freeText18412171562949112348" style="display:none">Lyrical, mesmerizing "faerie romance for men and women", thus far this story focuses on Anodos and his epic journey through the dreamlike Fairy Land - but if the reader is looking for tiny winged creatures, he will find them only briefly; Fairy Land is populated with numerous inhabitants who are in fact human, and others appear so but with supernatural qualities. Though society (and rampant marketing) have oversold the idea of a benign parallel world of beauty and frolicking sprites, make no mistake - the world McDonald has created is far more than pan flutes, babbling brooks and laughter, and much of the main character's adventure involves the grotesque and disturbing; Anodos definitely has his work cut out for him here. <br /><br />A wonderful, wonderful, story, with some of the most beautiful imagery I've read, and a glorious finish. Regarding the back-cover reference to a "faerie romance" - this is without question a love story, but not in the blushing, eyelid-fluttering sense of the word. Boys and men would do well to read this book, and will have plenty to rivet their attention. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18412171562949112348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText12781212374518428626" style="display:none">Like many of the other reviewers, I am certain that a second reading would reveal much more of this story to me. Many times throughtout the reading I wished I could just jump into MacDonalds mind and find the key to much that I am sure is allegorical! This book is so beautiful it almost hurts. I loved and was confused by it. I know now why C.S. Lewis thought him a master; if Lewis loooked up to him you know that most of the rest of us would see him as brilliant! <br /><br />The story begins with this young man's 21st birthday and continues through a meeting with humans and others who inhabit the world of faerie land into which he awakens. He travels through forest and caves, sea and river eventually making a great sacrifice for the purpose of revealing truth to a beloved friend who had been decieved. The man eventually awakens from his visit and is reminded to use all he learned in faerie land to help him live his life in reality. <br /><br />Not a difficult book to read, but definetly a book that bears much more study and thought than most! Recommend without reservations!</span>

I learned that he that will be a hero, will barely be a man; that he that will be nothing but a doer of his work, is sure of his manhood. In nothing was my ideal lowered, or dimmed, or grown less precious; I only saw it too plainly, to set myself for a moment beside it. Indeed, my ideal soon became my life; whereas, formerly, my life had consisted in a vain attempt to behold, if not my ideal in myself, at least myself in my ideal. </blockquote></span>

  <span id="freeText313170852326246251" style="display:none">What an excellent book. This is my favorite George MacDonald book by far. Along his journey, he encounters sinister Ash trees, mischievous kobolds, women who only appear in the reflection of mirrors, Sir Galahad, and a host of other fairy-folk. <div id="review_690389050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/690389050" />
    <a title="Michaela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7346032-michaela"><img alt="Michaela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346274846p2/7346032.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/690389050?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michaela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michaela" href="/user/show/7346032-michaela">Michaela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer690389050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11817607572913015755">Sometimes it seems like because we’re kept in suspense throughout a book and we’re still excited and caught up in the story when we finish the last chapter, we immediately give it five stars just for that, and then we either forget about it, or later realize it wasn’t all that good after all. In other words, it was fun while it lasted, but not worth a second read or even a second thought.<br/><br/>And then there are those that seem slower while we’re actually reading them, we’re tempted to quit halfway th</span>
  <span id="freeText11817607572913015755" style="display:none">Sometimes it seems like because we’re kept in suspense throughout a book and we’re still excited and caught up in the story when we finish the last chapter, we immediately give it five stars just for that, and then we either forget about it, or later realize it wasn’t all that good after all. In other words, it was fun while it lasted, but not worth a second read or even a second thought.<br /><br />And then there are those that seem slower while we’re actually reading them, we’re tempted to quit halfway through, not everything seems to connect very well, we’re not sure what the author is trying to say… And then we reach the end. And we sit there a few minutes trying to piece it all together. And months later we’re still thinking about some of the things the book said.<br /><br />Phantastes by George MacDonald is the latter kind.<br /><br />I’ve read some other George MacDonald books, and when I looked up reviews this always seemed to me to be the one most well-known of his fantasy works, so when I found it at a used bookstore I was excited. But one chapter in I was thinking, “Who is this guy and why is he wandering through… Fairyland?” But it was well worth every minute spent reading!<br /><br />In my opinion, Anodos is an extremely lucky person. He’s given a spiritual preview of what life is going to be like, starting when he wakes up one morning to find a stream flowing through his bedroom. He follows it into a strange land where many very odd things happen in seemingly no particular order, with even stranger people, some of whom are on their own journeys. I particularly love Sir Percival in his armor that will only shine again when the blows of enemy swords have chipped all the rust from it; and the strange house with the three doors - the door of grief, the door of sighs, and the door of dismay, from which the only door leading away is that of the vault of a tomb. And indeed, Anodos journeys through all kinds of fears and sorrows until his eventual painful death - but “The veil between, though very dark, is very thin.” And he finds himself soaring above all the sorrowful world below, and experiences more peace than he ever had before.<br /><br />His sudden return to life (in his own world) seems rather “to correspond to what we think death is, before we die.”<br /><br />He commences his ordinary life again, but with new hope: “I know that good is coming to me - that good is always coming; though few have at times the simplicity and the courage to believe it. What we call evil, is the only and best shape, which for the person and his condition at the time, could be assumed by the best good.”<br /><br />I’ve listed only a handful of the lovely parts of this book. It’s one that really calmed me down. These stories that don’t seem to make sense right away are always the best because I think so many of our thoughts seem incomprehensible even to our own minds, and it’s comforting to recognize them when they’re made just a little bit more coherent in someone else’s work. 5 stars, highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11817607572913015755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2654002994" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2654002994" />
    <a title="Elizabeth A.G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67736000-elizabeth-a-g"><img alt="Elizabeth A.G." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521007896p2/67736000.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2654002994?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth A.G." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth A.G." href="/user/show/67736000-elizabeth-a-g">Elizabeth A.G.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780856483493" href="/book/show/6692523-phantastes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2654002994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer845151815878620882">I was pleasantly surprised by this book, as I normally do not read much "fantasy" literature, but then the author, George MacDonald, did clarify this is not the usual children's fairy tale in the title, <u>Phantastes: A Fairie Romance for Men and Women</u>. The story centers around Anodos who has turned 21 and heir to the estate of his father, who has died. Anodos is given the key to his father's secretary and his journey begins upon opening a small drawer in that desk releasing an aged, miniscule and </span>
  <span id="freeText845151815878620882" style="display:none">I was pleasantly surprised by this book, as I normally do not read much "fantasy" literature, but then the author, George MacDonald, did clarify this is not the usual children's fairy tale in the title, <u>Phantastes: A Fairie Romance for Men and Women</u>. The story centers around Anodos who has turned 21 and heir to the estate of his father, who has died. Anodos is given the key to his father's secretary and his journey begins upon opening a small drawer in that desk releasing an aged, miniscule and magical woman. She was aware that the previous night, after reading a fairy tale, Anodos' sister asked, "is there a fairy-country, brother?" Anodos responded he "supposed there is, if one could find the way into it." This miniscule creature transformed herself into a beautiful woman to appease Anodos' doubts that she could grant his wish to "find the way" into Fairy Land. She told him he will find the way tomorrow and suddenly disappeared. The manner in which this spirit woman addresses Anodos indicates this is partly a coming-of-age story, as she calls him "foolish boy" and reprimands him by saying, "Is that all the philosophy you have gained in one-and-twenty years" and then she acccommodates herself to his "foolish prejudices" and changes her size. The next morning Anodos begins his wandering, rambling quest in Fairy Land where he finds magical trees, caves, human-like statues, knights, castles, giants, nymphs, and difficult adventures. Each adventure teaches him something new and he learns about true love, friendship, selflessness and humility. MacDonald's wonderful imagery lends to the mystery and magic of the story and he creates an anticipation and mood that keeps the reader's attention. I enjoyed the narrative form as well as the stories within the story to embellish this tale. It is also interesting that the name Anodos comes from the Greek meaning pathless or ascent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="845151815878620882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2050435217" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2050435217" />
    <a title="Beth Wangler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69177411-beth-wangler"><img alt="Beth Wangler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500412313p2/69177411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2050435217?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Wangler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Wangler" href="/user/show/69177411-beth-wangler">Beth Wangler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2050435217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10933107799478610900">I have such mixed feelings about this book, so I'm going to rate each third separately.<br/><br/>The beginning of "Phantastes": 3*<br/><br/>The book started off delightful and whimsical. We're drawn into a perplexing but beautiful faerie world with the narrator, Anados. It's lovely and leisurely and adventurous. I felt a little lost, since I had no previous exposure to faerie lore (my fairy experience is very Disney-fied), but it was okay, since Anados also didn't know a ton about what was happening. Though it was </span>
  <span id="freeText10933107799478610900" style="display:none">I have such mixed feelings about this book, so I'm going to rate each third separately.<br /><br />The beginning of "Phantastes": 3*<br /><br />The book started off delightful and whimsical. We're drawn into a perplexing but beautiful faerie world with the narrator, Anados. It's lovely and leisurely and adventurous. I felt a little lost, since I had no previous exposure to faerie lore (my fairy experience is very Disney-fied), but it was okay, since Anados also didn't know a ton about what was happening. Though it was pleasant, it wasn't exceptionally memorable, and there didn't seem to be a lot of meaning behind the early experiences.<br /><br />The middle: 2*<br /><br />I didn't hate this portion. It did start to drag, though. I eventually wearied of repetitive strange experiences that Anados had, which seemed to have little relation to each other, no predictable pattern. I also got frustrated with Anados, who seemed like a lustful teenager with no long-term memory. I almost put the book down entirely.<br /><br />The end: 5*<br /><br />I'm so glad I pushed through. All of a sudden, Phantastes turned beautiful. Truth after truth poured out of the pages, and all of the stories' messages were just what I needed to hear: "Past tears are present strength." "Rest is as needful as toil." "Better to...live in the love that floweth forth, than the love that cometh in."<br /><br />Oh, the ending was rapturously gorgeous. It left me with a sense of awe, a new appreciation for life, and a drive to serve others more heartily.<br /><br />Probably the ending was so beautiful because of the parts that came before. I just wish there had been more hints of the glory that was coming in the earlier sections, something to draw me forward and keep me intrigued.<br /><br />If you're looking for a book that takes commitment but will yield poignant fruit, this is the book for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10933107799478610900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_64804819" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64804819" />
    <a title="matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1324915-matthew"><img alt="matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1247356216p2/1324915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64804819?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64804819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13232195769062042780">This is an interesting book. C.S. Lewis cites MacDonald as his guru of types (note his role in the book "The Great Divorce"). Lewis further said that Phantastes "baptized [his:] imagination". Those are strong words and citations from an author that I love reading. So I decided to try out Phantastes. It is a "fairy romance", but really it is in the vein of Bunyan's "Pilgrim's Progress"--an extended allegory about life and philosophy. Except, in this version, none of the characters are explicitly </span>
  <span id="freeText13232195769062042780" style="display:none">This is an interesting book. C.S. Lewis cites MacDonald as his guru of types (note his role in the book "The Great Divorce"). Lewis further said that Phantastes "baptized [his:] imagination". Those are strong words and citations from an author that I love reading. So I decided to try out Phantastes. It is a "fairy romance", but really it is in the vein of Bunyan's "Pilgrim's Progress"--an extended allegory about life and philosophy. Except, in this version, none of the characters are explicitly named is an Pilgrim's Progress. It had a lot of beautiful language and some interesting ideas. However, parts of it I am not sure still what MacDonald was trying to say. I wish I understood more of his imagery. I need to go back and read it again. some day (it is a short read anyways).<br /><br />The tale is of a man who wakes up one day in fairy land. There, he wanders aimlessly until he finds a woman of marble that he brings to life by his song. He spends the rest of the book seeking her and learning along the way. It is a tale of innocence lost, humility found. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13232195769062042780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_589115703" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/589115703" />
    <a title="Kevin Finelli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19041295-kevin-finelli"><img alt="Kevin Finelli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367133193p2/19041295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/589115703?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Finelli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Finelli" href="/user/show/19041295-kevin-finelli">Kevin Finelli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19041295-kevin-finelli?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer589115703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17169401489108303691">I enjoyed Phantastes on several levels. At the surface, it is an enjoyable fantasy store or fairy tale about a young man who wakes up in the fairy world one day. The story is infused throughout with poetry, including a quote at the beginning of each chapter, and many songs and poems sung or spoken by the characters themselves. For me personally, hearing rather than reading helped me enjoy the poetry, which I probably would have skimmed through otherwise.<br/><br/>Beyond this, Phantastes is an allegory, th</span>
  <span id="freeText17169401489108303691" style="display:none">I enjoyed Phantastes on several levels. At the surface, it is an enjoyable fantasy store or fairy tale about a young man who wakes up in the fairy world one day. The story is infused throughout with poetry, including a quote at the beginning of each chapter, and many songs and poems sung or spoken by the characters themselves. For me personally, hearing rather than reading helped me enjoy the poetry, which I probably would have skimmed through otherwise.<br /><br />Beyond this, Phantastes is an allegory, that much becomes very obvious by the end of the book. However, it is not a hard allegory like Pilgrim's Progress, or even the Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe. It is not completely clear to me what all of the symbols represent, or even what the allegory as a whole is conveying. The most I can understand is that Phantastes tells the story of Anodos becoming his true self, a notion that MacDonald discusses at length in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/915566.Unspoken_Sermons_Series_I__II__III" title="Unspoken Sermons Series I, II, III by George MacDonald" rel="nofollow noopener">Unspoken Sermons</a>. He must contend with his shadow, which matches the idea of one's fallen, selfish nature. He serves as a squire to a great knight, and together they bear each other's burdens and travel about righting wrongs, similar to discipleship (in this case squire-ship) in Christ. The old woman in the cottage I think most closely represents God the Father, who, in their innermost natures show love most purely.<br /><br />It's a quick read, and probably worthy of a second look to try to understand the symbolism again. The book even contains some enjoyable short stories, like Cosmo and the Mirror, that stand well on their own.<br /><br />(note: after my second readthrough I have updated my rating from 4 to 5 stars. Someday I might update the review as well...)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17169401489108303691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2672068423" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2672068423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9160618340539252342">"Everywhere in Fairy Land forests are the places where one may most certainly expect adventures."<br/><br/>I loved the vivid descriptions in this fantasy journey of Anodos, but it was so meandering that I got bogged down in this relatively short read. Sometimes I felt I was hearing the same story over and over just in a slightly different light or setting. There were definitely some sections, I found more enchanting than others, but overall it was just OK. Maybe its because the fantasy genre has come so f</span>
  <span id="freeText9160618340539252342" style="display:none">"Everywhere in Fairy Land forests are the places where one may most certainly expect adventures."<br /><br />I loved the vivid descriptions in this fantasy journey of Anodos, but it was so meandering that I got bogged down in this relatively short read. Sometimes I felt I was hearing the same story over and over just in a slightly different light or setting. There were definitely some sections, I found more enchanting than others, but overall it was just OK. Maybe its because the fantasy genre has come so far since this early work. I was reading this as part of a group read, thank goodness. It kept me reading and "hearing" some different takes on what it all could mean.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9160618340539252342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63191123" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63191123" />
    <a title="Melinda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/611813-melinda"><img alt="Melinda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346297541p2/611813.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63191123?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melinda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melinda" href="/user/show/611813-melinda">Melinda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=christian">christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=worth-owning">worth-owning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=c-s-lewis">c-s-lewis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=british-lit">british-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/611813-melinda?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer63191123&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63191123" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16181215540673883921">I have received the 150th Annotated Edition of Phantastes, and have begun reading it again, this time with the helps provided by Nick Page. We will see how this time fares.<br/><br/>The annotated edition is very helpful. I recommend this version, I need to re-read and take notes... but I'm getting clearer on the levels in the book. It seems to be written along the ancient method of chiastic structure, which confuses me still. But at least I am aware of and searching for the elements in the chiastic struct</span>
  <span id="freeText16181215540673883921" style="display:none">I have received the 150th Annotated Edition of Phantastes, and have begun reading it again, this time with the helps provided by Nick Page. We will see how this time fares.<br /><br />The annotated edition is very helpful. I recommend this version, I need to re-read and take notes... but I'm getting clearer on the levels in the book. It seems to be written along the ancient method of chiastic structure, which confuses me still. But at least I am aware of and searching for the elements in the chiastic structure.<br /><br />My Nov 2009 Review **********************************************************************************************************<br />I read this book first in 1984. I did not understand it. I thought I would read it again, and perhaps being a bit better educated I would understand it better. I think I need to read it again in about 20 years.<br /><br />I love C.S. Lewis. C.S. Lewis credits the writing of George MacDonald with converting his imagination, years before his intellect or conscience was similarly converted. Lewis considered MacDonald his master, and even writes him into his book, "The Great Divorce", as a wonderful teacher. So if Lewis liked MacDonald and thought so highly of him, I am eager to understand and think so as well.<br /><br />Unfortunately, I really don't understand the book! It is like a huge mystical set of fairy tales, with shadows and behind-the-scenes stories that must mean something... but unfortunately I don't know what!<br /><br />In brief, Anodos, the main character, is transformed from this world into the land of Fairy. He has adventures, trials, temptations, and battles in the land of Fairy. What any of these adventures mean is difficult to say, either as individual adventures, or as one big huge adventure. In the end, ***SPOILER ALERT**** Anodos dies in a battle. However, his death in the land of Fairy merely transforms him back into the real world. So he lives the rest of his life using what he learned (I wish I knew what that was?) in this life.<br /><br />One wonderful quotation that I've always loved is this.... "I knew now, that it is by loving, and not by being loved, that one can come nearest the soul of another, yes, that where two love, it is the loving of each other, and not the being beloved by each other, that originates and perfects and assures their blessedness. I knew that love gives to him that loveth, power over any soul beloved, even if that soul know him not, bringing him inwardly close to that spirit, a power that cannot be but for good, for in proportion as selfishness intrudes, the love ceases, and the power which springs therefrom dies. Yet all love will, one day, meet with its return. All true love will, one day, behold its own image in the eyes of the beloved, and be humbly glad."<br /><br />So, someone read the book please, and share your thoughts on it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="16181215540673883921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>
          <a title="Classics and the Western Canon" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/19656451-phantastes---background-context-and-other-discussions">Classics and the ...:</a>
        </b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fa051Jy8s+Tb4SyHpUL8FvgOzh4casrXmg0/HGzkPS77FpXJNYDYuTalH5/IenRL97StMq/Y/D7hpOV+PqYXTg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rZsEQEJ6mqLuypMfbODXA+xEmifjQ6gV4Pkid2IX/0YrIKhd60bx/wOOoAcB2F9e4/75C1DxnvybUPgVMFXVJg==')})
  }

Responding to the evolutionary materialism of his contemporary (and antagonist<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17172076057824486306\" style=\"display:none\">What, if anything, is it that makes the human uniquely human? This, in part, is the question that G.K. Chesterton starts with in this classic exploration of human history. Responding to the evolutionary materialism of his contemporary (and antagonist) H.G. Wells, Chesterton in this work affirms human uniqueness and the unique message of the Christian faith. Writing in a time when social Darwinism was rampant, Chesterton instead argued that the idea that society has been steadily progressing from a state of primitivism and barbarity towards civilization is simply and flatly inaccurate. &quot;Barbarism and civilization were not successive stages in the progress of the world,&quot; he affirms, with arguments drawn from the histories of both Egypt and Babylon.  As always with Chesterton, there is in this analysis something (as he said of Blake) &quot;very plain and emphatic.&quot; He sees in Christianity a rare blending of philosophy and mythology, or reason and story, which satisfies both the mind and the heart. On both levels it rings true. As he puts it, &quot;in answer to the historical query of why it was accepted, and is accepted, I answer for millions of others in my reply; because it fits the lock; because it is like life.&quot; Here, as so often in Chesterton, we sense a lived, awakened faith. All that he writes derives from a keen intellect guided by the heart\'s own knowledge.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17172076057824486306\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return K. Chesterton, the \"Prince of Paradox,\" is at his witty best in this collection of twenty essays and articles from the turn of the twentieth century. Focusing on \"heretics\" — those who pride themselves on their superiority to Christian views — Che<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText907134082650926703\" style=\"display:none\">G. K. Chesterton, the &quot;Prince of Paradox,&quot; is at his witty best in this collection of twenty essays and articles from the turn of the twentieth century. Focusing on &quot;heretics&quot; — those who pride themselves on their superiority to Christian views — Chesterton appraises prominent figures who fall into that category from the literary and art worlds. Luminaries such as Rudyard Kipling, George Bernard Shaw, H. G. Wells, and James McNeill Whistler come under the author\'s scrutiny, where they meet with equal measures of his characteristic wisdom and good humor.In addition to incisive assessments of well-known individuals (&quot;Mr. Rudyard Kipling and Making the World Small&quot; and &quot;Mr. H. G. Wells and the Giants&quot;), these essays contain observations on the wider world. &quot;On Sandals and Simplicity,&quot; &quot;Science and the Savages,&quot; &quot;On Certain Modern Writers and the Institution of the Family,&quot; &quot;On Smart Novelists and the Smart Set,&quot; and &quot;Slum Novelists and the Slums&quot; reflect the main themes of Chesterton\'s life\'s work. Heretics roused the ire of some critics for censuring contemporary philosophies without providing alternatives; the author responded a few years later with a companion volume, Orthodoxy. Sardonic, jolly, and generous, both books are vintage Chesterton.\n\nHe is criticizing those who hold incomplete and inadequate views about &quot;life, the universe, and everything.&quot; He is, in short, criticizing all that host of non-Christian views of reality, as he demonstrated in his follow-up book Orthodoxy. The book is both an easy read and a difficult read. But he manages to demonstrate, among other things, that our new 21st century heresies are really not new because he himself deals with most of them.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"907134082650926703\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2413.George_MacDonald">About George MacDonald</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2413.George_MacDonald"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1201019294p3/2413.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2413.George_MacDonald">George MacDonald
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,681
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3821859745555064823">George MacDonald was a Scottish author, poet, and Christian minister.<br/><br/>He was educated at Aberdeen University and after a short and stormy career as a minister at Arundel, where his unorthodox views led to his dismissal, he turned to fiction as a means of earning a living. He wrote over 50 books.<br/><br/>Known particularly for his poignant fairy tales and fantasy novels, MacDonald inspired many authors, such</span>
  <span id="freeText3821859745555064823" style="display:none">George MacDonald was a Scottish author, poet, and Christian minister.<br /><br />He was educated at Aberdeen University and after a short and stormy career as a minister at Arundel, where his unorthodox views led to his dismissal, he turned to fiction as a means of earning a living. He wrote over 50 books.<br /><br />Known particularly for his poignant fairy tales and fantasy novels, MacDonald inspired many authors, such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7014283.G_K__Chesterton" title="G.K. Chesterton" rel="nofollow noopener">G.K. Chesterton</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/233593.W__H__Auden" title="W. H. Auden" rel="nofollow noopener">W. H. Auden</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/656983.J_R_R__Tolkien" title="J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">J.R.R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C__S__Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">C. S. Lewis</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/106.Madeleine_L_Engle" title="Madeleine L'Engle" rel="nofollow noopener">Madeleine L'Engle</a>. Lewis wrote that he regarded MacDonald as his "master": "Picking up a copy of <i>Phantastes</i> one day at a train-station bookstall, I began to read. A few hours later," said Lewis, "I knew that I had crossed a great frontier." G. K. Chesterton cited <i>The Princess and the Goblin</i> as a book that had "made a difference to my whole existence."<br /><br />Elizabeth Yates wrote of Sir Gibbie, "It moved me the way books did when, as a child, the great gates of literature began to open and first encounters with noble thoughts and utterances were unspeakably thrilling."<br /><br />Even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Mark Twain</a>, who initially disliked MacDonald, became friends with him, and there is some evidence that Twain was influenced by MacDonald.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_MacDonald">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_M...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3821859745555064823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2206809-phantastes-a-faerie-romance">Quotes from Phantastes: A Fae...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It is by loving, and not by being loved, that one can come nearest the soul of another; yea, that, where two love, it is the loving of each other, that originates and perfects and assures their blessedness. I knew that love gives to him that loveth, power over over any soul be loved, even if that soul know him not, bringing him inwardly close to that spirit; a power that cannot be but for good; for in proportion as selfishness intrudes, the love ceases, and the power which springs therefrom dies. Yet all love will, one day, meet with its return. &rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
